<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="b41b341d8a9544718dae142da7339771"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b41b341d8a9544718dae142da7339771">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd846b509-e2f7-44e4-8cd2-1cc9a4833445'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d57252819" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Integrated effects of migration channels broadening, charge density re-distribution, and oxygen vacancies modulation are achieved via high valence state ion-doping in case of Nb<sup>5+</sup>. The modified samples optimized excellent long-cycle stability, and superior low-temperature performance in the full cell. Importantly, it is demonstrated for the first time that high-valent transition metal preferentially replaces the Ti3 site of NTO, effectively improving electrical conductivity and ion diffusion rate. More interestingly, the “spring effect” of chemical bonding, which is twisted-recovered-twisted with the motion of Na<sup>+</sup>, is investigated for the first time by in situ Raman, and the stabilizing effect of Nb enables the more regular and reversible “spring effect”.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/99ee37b9-c57c-495f-b94f-63ac34378d99/anie202312310-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/99ee37b9-c57c-495f-b94f-63ac34378d99/anie202312310-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/95596493-9ff0-40c0-9d8e-2cccc5174204/anie202312310-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/99ee37b9-c57c-495f-b94f-63ac34378d99/anie202312310-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d57252823" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> (NTO) with high safety has been regarded as a promising anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries. In the present study, integrated modification of migration channels broadening, charge density re-distribution, and oxygen vacancies regulation are realized in case of Nb-doping and have obtained significantly enhanced cycling performance with 92 % reversible capacity retained after 3000 cycles at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, unexpected low-temperature performance with a high discharge capacity of 143 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under −15 °C is also achieved in the full cell. Theoretical investigation suggests that Nb preferentially replaces Ti3 sites, which effectively improves structural stability and lowers the diffusion energy barrier. What's more important, both the in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and in situ Raman furtherly confirm the robust spring effect of the Ti−O bond, making special charge compensation mechanism and respective regulation strategy to conquer the sluggish transport kinetics and low conductivity, which plays a key role in promoting electrochemical performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202312310-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202312310-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Due to the widespread availability of sodium, coupled with a rocking-chair mechanism and manufacturing process similar to those of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as promising candidates.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0001_R_d57252802e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a>-<a href="#anie202312310-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0003_R_d57252802e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the slow diffusion kinetics of large-sized Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) is becoming a barrier to the practical application of SIBs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0004_R_d57252802e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0005_R_d57252802e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span> especially in some extreme environments like low temperatures.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0006_R_d57252802e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#anie202312310-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0008_R_d57252802e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> (NTO), featuring an open crystal skeleton tunnel structure formed by interconnected TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, is an emerging anode with high theoretical capacity (198 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), overall stability, and high safety.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0009_R_d57252802e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0010_R_d57252802e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">10</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Unfortunately, poor ion/electron conductivity and sluggish transport kinetics restrict the practical capacity and rate performance of NTO.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0011_R_d57252802e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a>-<a href="#anie202312310-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0013_R_d57252802e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span> In response, many researchers are dedicated to addressing these issues with little success. For example, modulating morphology neglects the intrinsic conductivity,<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0014_R_d57252802e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Technol.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0015_R_d57252802e631" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Alloys Compd.">15</a></sup></span> surface coating cannot improve reaction kinetics,<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0016_R_d57252802e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0017_R_d57252802e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Mater. Lett.">17</a></sup></span> and hybridized structures are inappropriate for industrial scale-up for high costs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0009_R_d57252802e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0018_R_d57252802e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Electrochim. Acta">18</a></sup></span> The heteroelement doping strategy is an effective method for fundamentally modulating transport channels and electron distribution while stimulating oxygen vacancies to increase active sites.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0019_R_d57252802e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#anie202312310-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0024_R_d57252802e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Dalton Trans.">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Herein, to overcome the sluggish transport kinetics and low conductivity and unravel the related spring effects, we introduce Nb<sup>5+</sup> (64 pm vs Ti<sup>4+</sup> 60.5 pm)<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0025_R_d57252802e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">25</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0026_R_d57252802e649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span> with strong Nb−O bonds.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0027_R_d57252802e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span> The result indicates Nb<sup>5+</sup> can enhance conductivity, enlarge the migration channels, reduce the charge density in the channel, and accelerate ion migration(Scheme <a href="#anie202312310-fig-5001">1</a>) <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1</a>). The preferred site of Nb<sup>5+</sup> substitution for Ti<sup>4+</sup> is theoretically investigated to be the Ti3 site. In situ XRD and in situ Raman demonstrate continuous lattice variation and the robust spring effect of chemical bonding during Na<sup>+</sup> migration. Therefore, the Nb<sup>5+</sup>-substituted sample exhibits superior multiplicative performance of 118 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and attractive capacity retention of 92 % after 3000 cycles at 3000 A g<sup>−1</sup>. More importantly, the optimized NTO can give a high reversible capacity of 143 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under the low temperature of −15 °C in NTO||NVP full cell.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202312310-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/15bc6683-0e5a-4beb-86ba-9b51d42b9d31/anie202312310-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>

(f) Plots of (αhv)<sup>1/2</sup> vs photon energy (hv) and UV/Vis/DRs spectra of NTO and NTONb0.08 compounds. HR-TEM images of (g) NTO, (h) NTONb0.08.</p></div>

         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202312310-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202312310-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6-x</sub>Nb<sub>x</sub>O<sub>13</sub> (<i>x</i>=0, 0.04, 0.08, 0.12, marked as NTO, NTONb0.04, NTONb0.08, NTONb0.12) was synthesized by solid-phase method. The atomic ratios are in general agreement with the theoretical values according to the inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometer results (ICP-MS, Table S1). XRD peaks are consistent with the monoclinic crystal system (C2/m space group, JCPDS, No. 73-1398) (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1a</a>). But additional phase NaNbO<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS, No. 77-0873) appears in NTONb0.12 (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1b</a>). Furthermore, the (200) and (−201) peaks move slightly to lower angles with increased Nb, implying lattice extension. XRD refinements results show that the Nb<sup>5+</sup> substitution promotes the oxygen vacancies formation for charge balance (Figure S1). Moreover, the cell parameters (<i>a</i>, <i>c</i>, <i>V</i>) and Na−O bond length increase sequentially with increased Nb content, which could provide more storage sites and widens Na<sup>+</sup> transport paths (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1c</a>, Table S2).</p>
         
         <p>The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of Nb 3d in NTONb0.08 shows the existence of Nb in the +5 oxidation state (Figure S2). The Ti 2p spectrum of NTONb0.08 exhibits additional Ti<sup>3+</sup> peaks as against the pristine NTO, indicating a partial Ti<sup>4+</sup> reduction (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1d</a>). In addition, the new peak attributed to the oxygen-deficient region appeared in the O 1s spectrum of NTONb0.08, resulting from the inability of the lattice oxygen electrons to balance the increased positive charge (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1e</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0028_R_d57252802e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span> Electron concentration in the conduction band increases in NTONb0.08 compared to NTO, and the band gap energy decreases significantly from 3.551 to 3.472 eV according to the Ultraviolet-visible diffuse reflectance spectroscopy (UV/Vis/DRs) results (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1f</a>). All the aforementioned results demonstrate that Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping can alter the charge distribution, induce oxygen vacancy, and narrow the, thus strengthening the electronic and ionic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0029_R_d57252802e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Storage Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0030_R_d57252802e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span> Raman spectra indicate NTONb0.08 has an insignificant spike at 250.07 cm<sup>−1</sup> relevant to the Nb−O stretching vibration (Figure S3). The Ti−O is blue-shifted in Fourier Transform Infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy of NTONb0.08 compared to NTO, revealing the partial substitution of Ti by Nb with different ionic radii and outer electronic structures, thus inducing a change in the dipole moment during the molecular vibration (Figure S4).</p>
         
         <p>Field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) results indicate that Nb doping did not change the original morphology and grain size (Figure S5). As to high-resolution TEM results, NTO shows lattice stripe distances of 0.299 and 0.266 nm, corresponding to (310) and (402) planes (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1g</a>). Similarly, NTONb0.08 reveals larger lattice spatial distances of 0.301 and 0.629 nm, matching well with the (310) and (−201) planes (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0001">1h</a>), further disclosing that the Nb<sup>5+</sup> substitution successfully expands the NTO skeletal layer spacing. In addition, energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping is performed on NTONb0.08 shows Na, Ti, O, and Nb, are uniformly dispersed in the material (Figure S6).<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0031_R_d57252802e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nature">31</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>NTO shows relatively poor cycling performance at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> with only 61 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles corresponding to 85.6 % capacity retention (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure S8). As to NTONb0.08, the retrievable capacity increases to 114 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1400 cycles (91.3 % capacity retention), yet that of NTONb0.12 decreases to 93 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. NTONb0.08 displays the best cycling performance with 115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 92 % after 3000 cycles at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, with a final capacity of 115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2c</a>). Moreover, NTONb0.08 also exhibits the most superior rate performance, with a 55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> higher capacity than NTO at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and a 43 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> higher at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2b</a>). The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves at different current rates show that NTONb0.08 has a smaller polarization than NTO with the increased sodium storage sites and the rapid migration of Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure S9). More interestingly, NTONb0.08 possesses a higher reversible capacity (103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) than that (42.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) of NTO after 200 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under low temperature of −15 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2d</a>). Furthermore, NTONb0.08 maintains a reversible capacity of 135 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under 60 °C (Figure S10). The excellent electrochemical properties of NTONb0.08 over a wide range of temperatures indicate that it is a suitable anode material for multi-scenario applications.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202312310-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7faf4c77-f07b-4f54-8c8f-72378a0cc95e/anie202312310-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7faf4c77-f07b-4f54-8c8f-72378a0cc95e/anie202312310-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7bb2d24c-8c43-4b7b-9035-a7b9075a8752/anie202312310-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7faf4c77-f07b-4f54-8c8f-72378a0cc95e/anie202312310-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202312310-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202312310" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Cycling and (b) rate performance comparison of NTONb<sub>x</sub> samples. (c) Long-term cycling with 3000 cycles of NTONb0.08 at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. (d) Low-temperature cycling performance of NTO and NTONb0.08 at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under −15 °C. (e) The capacity retention, the inset is an array of commercial LEDs for the SCU logo powered by NTONb0.08||NVP, (f) rate performance, energy density g) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) curves, and the corresponding sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) (g) of NTO||NVP and NTONb0.08||NVP under −15 °C. (h) The low-temperature performance of NTONb0.08||NVP and other recent reports.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further evaluate the practical applications, the full cells are assembled with sodium vanadium phosphate (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NVP) (Figure S11). The low-temperature performance was estimated under −15 °C. And the NTONb0.08||NVP also displays better stability with higher capacity retention of 73 % than that (61 %) of NTO||NVP, powering an SCU icon consisting of an array of 102 commercial red light-emitting diodes (LEDs) (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2e</a>). NTO||NVP exhibits a capacity of 128 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 mA g<sup>−1</sup> but almost no capacity at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (calculated based on the mass of active material of the negative electrode). In contrast, NTONb0.08||NVP delivers a high specific capacity of 179, 143, 108, and 42 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20, 100, 200, and 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, representing specific energy of 154, 114, 77, and 23 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (calculated based on the total mass of active material on the positive and negative electrodes) (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2f</a>). The excellent multiplicity performance of NTONb0.08||NVP at low temperatures is attributed to its higher overall Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient than that of NTO||NVP (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2g</a>). More remarkably, the low-temperature performance of NTONb0.08||NVP is in a favorable position compared with existing reports of SIBs (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0002">2h</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0006_R_d57252802e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0010_R_d57252802e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">10</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0032_R_d57252802e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nat. Commun.">32</a>-<a href="#anie202312310-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0036_R_d57252802e936" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Sci.">36</a></sup></span> Additionally, NTONb0.08||NVP also exhibits superior rate performance and cycling stability compared with NTO||NVP under 25 °C (Figure S14–15).</p>
         
         <p>The reduction peaks in the first-cycle cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NTO and NTONb0.08 disappeared in subsequent cycles and are mainly attributed to irreversible capacity and formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202312310-fig-0003">b</a>). In the third cycle, a pair of peaks at 0.736/0.927 V corresponding to the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox reaction shows a smaller polarization voltage (191 mV) than that (201 mV) of NTO, which is consistent with the enhanced conductivity and reaction kinetics. The similar GCD curves for the first three cycles reveal that Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping did not change the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction mechanism. More interestingly, the initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) was effectively enhanced from 55.26 % for NTO to 79.54 % for NTONb0.08 (Figure S16). And the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient 1.22*10<sup>−15</sup>–3.61*10<sup>−11</sup> of NTONb0.08 is about ten times higher than that 1.81*10<sup>−16</sup>–8.52*10<sup>−12</sup> of NTO, indicating Nb<sup>5+</sup> substitution promotes the reaction kinetics (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0003">3c</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202312310-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a4edb731-fa24-4b45-bcde-a65c45491e63/anie202312310-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a4edb731-fa24-4b45-bcde-a65c45491e63/anie202312310-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a8dd87b2-1fdf-4080-968c-355b2ed162e6/anie202312310-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a4edb731-fa24-4b45-bcde-a65c45491e63/anie202312310-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202312310-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202312310" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The first three CV curves of (a) NTO and (b) NTONb0.08 at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> under 25 °C. (c) GITT curves and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of NTO and NTONb0.08 under 25 °C. (d) Comparison of diffusion contribution ratios and capacitive storage mechanisms in NTONb0.08 from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. EIS curves of (e) NTO and (f) NTONb0.08 after 1 cycle, 50 cycles, and 200 cycles under 25 °C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Moreover, the charge transport kinetics of NTONb0.08 is studied based on CV analysis with varied scan rates from 0.1 to 0.5 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Well-maintained shape with an increasing scan rate suggests small polarization (Figure S17a). Calculated <i>b</i>-values are 0.89/0.74 for redox reactions, meaning a Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetic dominated by the diffusion-controlled response and capacitive contribution in concert (Figure S17b).<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0037_R_d57252802e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span> The capacitive contribution rises from 68 % to 83 % as the scan rate increases, demonstrating pseudocapacitive charge storage mechanism achieves high-rate energy storage (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0003">3d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0038_R_d57252802e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Commun.">38</a></sup></span> Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0003">3e</a>–<a href="#anie202312310-fig-0003">f</a>) traces impedance changes, fitting an equivalent circuit model of Figure S18a to obtain the charge transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0039_R_d57252802e1033" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">39</a></sup></span> Compared with the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of 128.3, 96.77, and 80.38 for NTO at the 1<sup>st</sup>, 50<sup>th</sup>, and 200<sup>th</sup> cycles, respectively, those of NTONb0.08 with 47.32, 40.01, and 14.51 are much lower, suggesting that the Na<sup>+</sup> transport rate and the interfacial stability have been improved (Figure S18b).</p>
         
         <p>Figure S22 shows the optimized structure model representing NTO and the substitution of different Ti sites (named NTNO-site1, NTNO-site2, and NTNO-site3 respectively). Expansion of cell parameters after substitution indicates enlargement of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels (Table S9). Negative formation energies of three substitution sites calculated by density functional theory (DFT) prove thermodynamically more favorable and structurally more stable, and the Ti3 site is the preferred site with the lowest formation energy (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4a</a>). The total density of states (TDOS) and partial density of states (PDOS) exhibit conduction band shifts toward lower energy as Nb contributes to the Fermi level (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4b</a>–<a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">c</a> and Figure S23). Furthermore, NTNO-site3 possesses the narrowest band gap and the highest DOS intensity on the conduction band side, indicating the most advantageous electrical conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0040_R_d57252802e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Interdiscip. Mater.">40</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0041_R_d57252802e1062" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">41</a></sup></span> In summary, with the most stabilized structure and the most excellent conductivity, we may consider NTNO-site3 as the best substitution model. For convenience, NTNO-site3 will be abbreviated as NTNO.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202312310-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fb336585-5df7-4a2a-8967-bc078e8a436c/anie202312310-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fb336585-5df7-4a2a-8967-bc078e8a436c/anie202312310-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/699cc13d-266b-4bed-a117-2fd50606f073/anie202312310-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fb336585-5df7-4a2a-8967-bc078e8a436c/anie202312310-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202312310-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202312310" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Formation energies of NTNO-site1, NTNO-site2, and NTNO-site3. Partial density of states (PDOS) of Na, O, Ti, and Nb orbitals with <i>x</i>=0 for (b) NTO, (c) NTNO-site3. (d) Bader charge of NTO and NTNO. Charge density distribution for (e) NTO, (f) NTNO. (g) The adsorption energy of Na in NTO and NTNO. (h) The corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barriers of NTO and NTNO. Schematic illustrations of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path in structural models of (i) NTO, (j) NTNO. (k) Charge density distribution for NTO and NTNO with Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion to step 5, step 7, and step 8.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Bader charge analysis shows partial electrons of Nb in NTNO transfer to surrounding Ti, which reduces the average valence state of Ti, thus improving the electrical conductivity (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0042_R_d57252802e1103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Commun.">42</a></sup></span> Additionally, charge density near Nb and oxygen vacancies of NTNO decreases, facilitating the reduction of electron binding in Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4e</a>–<a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">f</a>, Figure S24). Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energies reflecting the sodium affinity of material, are calculated corresponding to three sites in Figure S25, respectively. NTNO has stronger adsorption energy than NTO, indicating its excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance and structural stabilization after repeated ion insertion/extraction (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, we estimate Nb substitution effects on multiplicative performance and cycling stability by calculating the energy barrier (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> migration along the <i>b</i>-axis (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4h</a>). The Na<sup>+</sup> moves along the path from Na1 to Na14 as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4i</a>–<a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">j</a>. Compared with the maximum <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of 1.803 eV to be overcome for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in NTO, NTNO only encounters a small <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of 1.329 eV, verifying an improvement of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0043_R_d57252802e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">43</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0044_R_d57252802e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">44</a></sup></span> Electron charge density in the diffusion path demonstrates the weaker electron binding and smaller effect of Na<sup>+</sup> motion on surrounding atoms in NTNO than in NTO (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0004">4k</a>). The charge density GIF plot dynamics in Figure S26 prove more clearly that NTNO, because of its wider diffusion channel, allows smoother migration of Na<sup>+</sup> with mitigating impacts on the crystal structure and electronic structure. Therefore, the broadened diffusion channel, increased electrical conductivity, and faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport of NTNO are expected to make it a new generation of anode materials with high capacity, excellent multiplicity performance, and superior cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate lattice changes of NTO and NTONb0.08 electrodes (using Na metal as counter/reference electrode), in situ XRD tests are performed to monitor dynamic diffraction peak shifts during the complete discharge-charge–discharge process in 0.01–2.5 V (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0005">5a</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0005">5c</a>). The regular shifts of peaks with no new peaks appearing, indicate a solid solution storage mechanism.<span><sup><a href="#anie202312310-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202312310-bib-0045_R_d57252802e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Inorg. Chem.">45</a></sup></span> With the beginning insertion of additional Na<sup>+</sup> during the first discharge, a much more apparent variation in peak position and intensity in NTONb0.08 compared to NTO indicates that Na<sup>+</sup> inserts more easily and migrates at a faster rate (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0005">5b</a>, <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0005">5d</a>). Upon gradual discharge to low-voltage, the (200) peak moves to a higher angle, corresponding to the contraction of lattice parameters <i>a</i>. (110) and (020) peaks shift to a lower angle, which indicates lattice parameters <i>b</i> and <i>c</i> expand, owing to the increase in ionic radius caused by Ti<sup>4+</sup> electrochemical reduction and spacing expansion caused by Na<sup>+</sup> occupation. Notably, there is a greater sustained shifting for NTONb0.08 than NTO, due to more Na<sup>+</sup> inserts and more capacity delivers. In addition, NTONb0.08 peak intensity decreases more modestly, suggesting that it is possible to accommodate more Na<sup>+</sup> with less structural impact. As Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted, NTO has a more irreversible capacity than NTONb0.08 with larger volume changes (Figure S27). Discharging again to 0.01 V, the almost constant crystal volume of NTONb0.08 is attributed to the broadened diffusion channel, reduced charge density, and stabilizing action of strong Nb−O bond that enables Na<sup>+</sup> to be readily insertion/exclusion.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202312310-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/578daa5c-8277-4871-9270-3bde17102592/anie202312310-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/578daa5c-8277-4871-9270-3bde17102592/anie202312310-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b52966b2-30f0-4014-bc52-eb48460b93a9/anie202312310-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/578daa5c-8277-4871-9270-3bde17102592/anie202312310-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202312310-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202312310" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Lattice evolution of NTO under 25 °C. (a) In situ XRD patterns corresponding to discharge-charge–discharge curves between 0.01–2.5 V. (b) Enlarged XRD spectra of (200), (110), and (020) crystal planes. Lattice evolution of NTONb0.08. (c) In situ XRD patterns corresponding to discharge-charge–discharge curves between 0.01–2.5 V. (d) Enlarged XRD spectra of (200), (110), and (020) crystal planes. In situ Raman patterns of NTONb0.08 correspond to (e) the 2<sup>nd</sup> charge/discharge curve, and (f) the first six cycles between 0.01–2.5 V.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Subsequently, in situ Raman tests investigate surface structure change of NTO and NTONb0.08, showing peak intensity of Ti−O−Ti, Na−O−Ti, and Ti−O bonds enhancing or weakening along with Na<sup>+</sup> inserting (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0005">5e</a> and Figure S28a). This phenomenon called the “spring effect” is manifested as a distortion-restoration-distortion of the chemical bond, due to electrostatic attraction/repulsion between migrating Na<sup>+</sup> and ions around the tunnel. In particular, the more regular and reversible “spring effect” in NTONb0.08 be accounted for by broadened channel weakening coulomb force, on the other hand by robust Nb−O bonding and increased Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites reducing the rigid strain of crystal structure. Moreover, the regular variation of the Raman peak intensity of NTONb0.08 in the first six cycles also corresponds to the spring effect of the Ti−O bond. Although the peak intensity decay at the 4<sup>th</sup>–5<sup>th</sup> cycles, it almost returns to the initial state after six cycles, which is consistent with an activation process of the material. And the peak position is hardly shifted, which is consistent with superior long-cycle stability (Figure <a href="#anie202312310-fig-0005">5f</a>). In contrast, an irreversible peak position and intensity change in NTO indicates the irreversible structure destruction by Na<sup>+</sup>, which may be one of the reasons for low ICE (Figure S28b).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202312310-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202312310-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we ingeniously designed an NTONb0.08 anode with channel broadening, oxygen vacancies regulation, and charge density redistribution via the introduction of Nb<sup>5+</sup>. On account of improved conductivity and diffusion kinetics, NTONb0.08 exhibits remarkable electrochemical characteristics with 92 % capacity retention after 3000 cycles at 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under 25 °C, with a final charge capacity of 115 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. And excellent low-temperature performance with a high discharge capacity of 143 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> under −15 °C is also achieved in NTONb0.08||NVP. A systematic study demonstrated that the preferential substitution of Ti3, efficiently reduces the electron charge density and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier in the channel with more active sites. More interestingly, in situ XRD and in situ Raman revealed that the loss of capacity in the first cycle of NTO and the capacity decay over long cycles could be attributed to the irregular and irreversible spring effect of the chemical bonding in the Na<sup>+</sup> movement. And the robust spring effect of NTONb0.08 due to the solidifying of Nb−O bonding plays a key role in improving the stability.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
